**Johann Carl Friedrich Gauss** (;[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-2) German: _Gauß_[[kaʁl ˈfʁiːdʁɪç ˈɡaʊs]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German "Help:IPA/Standard German")[](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/a1/De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg/De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg.mp3 "Play audio")[ⓘ](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg "File:De-carlfriedrichgauss.ogg");[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-3)[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-4)[Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_language "Latin language"): _Carolus Fridericus Gauss_; 30 April 1777– 23 February 1855) was a German [mathematician](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematician "Mathematician"), [astronomer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomer "Astronomer"), [geodesist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesy "Geodesy"), and [physicist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physicist "Physicist"), who contributed to many fields in mathematics and science. He was director of the [Göttingen Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Observatory "Göttingen Observatory") in Germany and professor of astronomy from 1807 until his death in 1855.

While studying at the [University of Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen "University of Göttingen"), he propounded several mathematical [theorems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorem "Theorem"). As an independent scholar, he wrote the [masterpieces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masterpiece "Masterpiece")_[Disquisitiones Arithmeticae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disquisitiones\_Arithmeticae "Disquisitiones Arithmeticae")_ and _Theoria motus corporum coelestium_. Gauss produced the second and third complete proofs of the [fundamental theorem of algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra "Fundamental theorem of algebra"). In [number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory "Number theory"), he made numerous contributions, such as the [composition law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_composition_law "Gauss composition law"), the [law of quadratic reciprocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity "Quadratic reciprocity") and one case [[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-5) of the [Fermat polygonal number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_polygonal_number_theorem "Fermat polygonal number theorem"). He also contributed to the theory of binary and ternary quadratic forms, the construction of the [heptadecagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heptadecagon "Heptadecagon"), and the theory of [hypergeometric series](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypergeometric_function "Hypergeometric function"). Due to Gauss' extensive and fundamental contributions to science and mathematics, more than [100 mathematical and scientific concepts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_things_named_after_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss "List of things named after Carl Friedrich Gauss") are named after him.

Gauss was instrumental in the identification of [Ceres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet) "Ceres (dwarf planet)") as a dwarf planet. His work on the motion of planetoids disturbed by large planets led to the introduction of the [Gaussian gravitational constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_gravitational_constant "Gaussian gravitational constant") and the [method of least squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_squares "Least squares"), which he had discovered before [Adrien-Marie Legendre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre "Adrien-Marie Legendre") published it. Gauss led the geodetic survey of the Kingdom of Hanover together with an arc measurement project from 1820 to 1844; he was one of the founders of [geophysics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geophysics "Geophysics") and formulated the fundamental principles of [magnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetism "Magnetism"). His practical work led to the invention of the [heliotrope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliotrope_(instrument) "Heliotrope (instrument)") in 1821, a [magnetometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetometer "Magnetometer") in 1833 and – with [Wilhelm Eduard Weber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber "Wilhelm Eduard Weber") – the first electromagnetic [telegraph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telegraphy "Telegraphy") in 1833.

Gauss was the first to discover and study [non-Euclidean geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry "Non-Euclidean geometry"), which he also named. He developed a [fast Fourier transform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fast_Fourier_transform "Fast Fourier transform") some 160 years before [John Tukey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Tukey "John Tukey") and [James Cooley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Cooley "James Cooley").

Gauss refused to publish incomplete work and left several works to be edited [posthumously](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Posthumous_publication "Posthumous publication"). He believed that the act of learning, not possession of knowledge, provided the greatest enjoyment. Gauss was not a committed or enthusiastic teacher, generally preferring to focus on his own work. Nevertheless, some of his students, such as [Dedekind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Dedekind "Richard Dedekind") and [Riemann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann "Bernhard Riemann"), became well-known and influential mathematicians in their own right.

### Youth and education

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Youth and education")]

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Braunschweig_Brunswick_Geburtshaus_CF_Gauss_%281914%29.jpg/250px-Braunschweig_Brunswick_Geburtshaus_CF_Gauss_%281914%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Braunschweig_Brunswick_Geburtshaus_CF_Gauss_(1914).jpg)

Birthplace in [Brunswick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braunschweig "Braunschweig") (destroyed in World War II)

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Goe.Kurze.Geismarstr.Gauss.Wohnhaus.JPG/250px-Goe.Kurze.Geismarstr.Gauss.Wohnhaus.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Goe.Kurze.Geismarstr.Gauss.Wohnhaus.JPG)

Gauss's home as student in [Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen "Göttingen")

Gauss was born on 30 April 1777 in [Brunswick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braunschweig "Braunschweig"), in the [Duchy of Brunswick-Wolfenbüttel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duchy_of_Brunswick-Wolfenb%C3%BCttel "Duchy of Brunswick-Wolfenbüttel") (now in the German state of [Lower Saxony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower_Saxony "Lower Saxony")). His family was of relatively low social status.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-6) His father Gebhard Dietrich Gauss (1744–1808) worked variously as a butcher, bricklayer, gardener, and treasurer of a death-benefit fund. Gauss characterized his father as honourable and respected, but rough and dominating at home. He was experienced in writing and calculating, whereas his second wife Dorothea, Carl Friedrich's mother, was nearly illiterate.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington20048-7) He had one elder brother from his father's first marriage.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington20048%E2%80%939-8)

Gauss was a [child prodigy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Child_prodigy "Child prodigy") in mathematics. When the elementary teachers noticed his intellectual abilities, they brought him to the attention of the [Duke of Brunswick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_William_Ferdinand,_Duke_of_Brunswick "Charles William Ferdinand, Duke of Brunswick") who sent him to the local _Collegium Carolinum_,[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-10) which he attended from 1792 to 1795 with [Eberhard August Wilhelm von Zimmermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eberhard_August_Wilhelm_von_Zimmermann "Eberhard August Wilhelm von Zimmermann") as one of his teachers.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger193310-11)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200414-12)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Ullrich-13) Thereafter the Duke granted him the resources for studies of mathematics, sciences, and [classical languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classics "Classics") at the [University of Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen "University of Göttingen") until 1798.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-scientificmonthly-14) His professor in mathematics was [Abraham Gotthelf Kästner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham_Gotthelf_K%C3%A4stner "Abraham Gotthelf Kästner"), whom Gauss called "the leading mathematician among poets, and the leading poet among mathematicians" because of his [epigrams](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epigram "Epigram").[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200424-15)[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-16) Astronomy was taught by [Karl Felix Seyffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Felix_Seyffer "Karl Felix Seyffer"), with whom Gauss stayed in correspondence after graduation;[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200426-17)[Olbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Matthias_Olbers "Heinrich Wilhelm Matthias Olbers") and Gauss mocked him in their correspondence.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-18) On the other hand, he thought highly of [Georg Christoph Lichtenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Christoph_Lichtenberg "Georg Christoph Lichtenberg"), his teacher of physics, and of [Christian Gottlob Heyne](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Gottlob_Heyne "Christian Gottlob Heyne"), whose lectures in classics Gauss attended with pleasure.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200426-17) Fellow students of this time were [Johann Friedrich Benzenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Benzenberg "Johann Friedrich Benzenberg"), [Farkas Bolyai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Farkas_Bolyai "Farkas Bolyai"), and [Heinrich Wilhelm Brandes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Brandes "Heinrich Wilhelm Brandes").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200426-17)

He was likely a self-taught student in mathematics since he independently rediscovered several theorems.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Ullrich-13) He solved a geometrical problem that had occupied mathematicians since the [Ancient Greeks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece") when he determined in 1796 which regular [polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon") can be constructed by [compass and straightedge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions "Compass and straightedge constructions"). This discovery ultimately led Gauss to choose mathematics instead of [philology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philology "Philology") as a career.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200428-19) Gauss's mathematical diary, a collection of short remarks about his results from the years 1796 until 1814, shows that many ideas for his mathematical magnum opus [Disquisitiones Arithmeticae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disquisitiones_Arithmeticae "Disquisitiones Arithmeticae") (1801) date from this time.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200437-20)

As an elementary student, Gauss and his class were tasked by their teacher, J.G. Büttner, to sum the numbers from 1 to 100. Much to Büttner's surprise, Gauss replied with the correct answer of 5050 in a vastly faster time than expected.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-21) Gauss had realised that the sum could be rearranged as 50 pairs of 101 (1+100=101, 2+99=101, etc.). Thus, he simply multiplied 50 by 101.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-22) Other accounts state that he computed the sum as 100 sets of 101 and divided by 2.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-23)

Gauss graduated as a [Doctor of Philosophy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy "Doctor of Philosophy") in 1799, not in Göttingen, as is sometimes stated,[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-25)[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Marsden-24) but at the Duke of Brunswick's special request from the University of Helmstedt, the only state university of the duchy. [Johann Friedrich Pfaff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Pfaff "Johann Friedrich Pfaff") assessed his doctoral thesis, and Gauss got the degree _[in absentia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graduation "Graduation")_ without further oral examination.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Ullrich-13) The Duke then granted him the cost of living as a private scholar in Brunswick. Gauss subsequently refused calls from the [Russian Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences "Russian Academy of Sciences") in [St. Peterburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Peterburg "St. Peterburg") and [Landshut University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Maximilian_University_of_Munich "Ludwig Maximilian University of Munich").[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich2000-26)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Beuermann-27) Later, the Duke promised him the foundation of an observatory in Brunswick in 1804. Architect [Peter Joseph Krahe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Joseph_Krahe "Peter Joseph Krahe") made preliminary designs, but one of [Napoleon's wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_of_the_Fourth_Coalition "War of the Fourth Coalition") cancelled those plans:[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-28) the Duke was killed in the [battle of Jena](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_Jena%E2%80%93Auerstedt "Battle of Jena–Auerstedt") in 1806. The duchy was abolished in the following year, and Gauss's financial support stopped.

When Gauss was calculating asteroid orbits in the first years of the century, he established contact with the astronomical communities of [Bremen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bremen "Bremen") and [Lilienthal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lilienthal,_Lower_Saxony "Lilienthal, Lower Saxony"), especially [Wilhelm Olbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Olbers "Wilhelm Olbers"), [Karl Ludwig Harding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Ludwig_Harding "Karl Ludwig Harding"), and [Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Bessel "Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel"), forming part of the informal group of astronomers known as the [Celestial police](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_police "Celestial police").[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200450,_54%E2%80%9355,_74%E2%80%9377-29) One of their aims was the discovery of further planets. They assembled data on asteroids and comets as a basis for Gauss's research on their orbits, which he later published in his astronomical magnum opus _[Theoria motus corporum coelestium](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Theoria\_motus\_corporum\_coelestium&action=edit&redlink=1 "Theoria motus corporum coelestium (page does not exist)")_ (1809).[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200491%E2%80%9392-30)

### Professor in Göttingen

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Professor in Göttingen")]

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Goettingen_Sternwarte_01.jpeg/250px-Goettingen_Sternwarte_01.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Goettingen_Sternwarte_01.jpeg)

Old Göttingen observatory, c. 1800

In November 1807, Gauss was hired by the [University of Göttingen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen "University of Göttingen"), then an institution of the newly founded [Kingdom of Westphalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Westphalia "Kingdom of Westphalia") under [Jérôme Bonaparte](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Bonaparte "Jérôme Bonaparte"), as full professor and director of the [astronomical observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Observatory "Göttingen Observatory"),[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200485%E2%80%9387-31) and kept the chair until his death in 1855. He was soon confronted with the demand for two thousand [francs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franc "Franc") from the Westphalian government as a war contribution, which he could not afford to pay. Both Olbers and [Laplace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace "Pierre-Simon Laplace") wanted to help him with the payment, but Gauss refused their assistance. Finally, an anonymous person from [Frankfurt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frankfurt "Frankfurt"), later discovered to be [Prince-primate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prince-primate "Prince-primate")[Dalberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Theodor_Anton_Maria_von_Dalberg "Karl Theodor Anton Maria von Dalberg"),[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200486%E2%80%9387-32) paid the sum.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200485%E2%80%9387-31)

Gauss took on the directorship of the 60-year-old observatory, founded in 1748 by [Prince-elector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prince-elector "Prince-elector")[George II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_II_of_Great_Britain "George II of Great Britain") and built on a converted fortification tower,[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel192981%E2%80%9382-33) with usable, but partly out-of-date instruments.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel192949-34) The construction of a new observatory had been approved by Prince-elector [George III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_III "George III") in principle since 1802, and the Westphalian government continued the planning,[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel192983-35) but Gauss could not move to his new place of work until September 1816.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Beuermann-27) He got new up-to-date instruments, including two [meridian circles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meridian_circle "Meridian circle") from [Repsold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Georg_Repsold "Johann Georg Repsold")[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel192984-36) and [Reichenbach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Friedrich_von_Reichenbach "Georg Friedrich von Reichenbach"),[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929119-37) and a [heliometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliometer "Heliometer") from [Fraunhofer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_von_Fraunhofer "Joseph von Fraunhofer").[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel192956-38)

The scientific activity of Gauss, besides pure mathematics, can be roughly divided into three periods: astronomy was the main focus in the first two decades of the 19th century, geodesy in the third decade, and physics, mainly magnetism, in the fourth decade.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein19797-39)

Gauss made no secret of his aversion to giving academic lectures.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich2000-26)[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Beuermann-27) But from the start of his academic career at Göttingen, he continuously gave lectures until 1854.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004405%E2%80%93410-40) He often complained about the burdens of teaching, feeling that it was a waste of his time. On the other hand, he occasionally described some students as talented.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich2000-26) Most of his lectures dealt with astronomy, geodesy, and [applied mathematics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Applied_mathematics "Applied mathematics"),[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Wittmann-41) and only three lectures on subjects of pure mathematics.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich2000-26)[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-42) Some of Gauss's students went on to become renowned mathematicians, physicists, and astronomers: [Moritz Cantor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moritz_Cantor "Moritz Cantor"), [Dedekind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Dedekind "Richard Dedekind"), [Dirksen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enno_Dirksen "Enno Dirksen"), [Encke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Franz_Encke "Johann Franz Encke"), [Gould](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Apthorp_Gould "Benjamin Apthorp Gould"),[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-44)[Heine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eduard_Heine "Eduard Heine"), [Klinkerfues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Klinkerfues "Ernst Klinkerfues"), [Kupffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adolph_Theodor_Kupffer "Adolph Theodor Kupffer"), [Listing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Benedict_Listing "Johann Benedict Listing"), [Möbius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/August_Ferdinand_M%C3%B6bius "August Ferdinand Möbius"), [Nicolai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Bernhard_Gottfried_Nicolai "Friedrich Bernhard Gottfried Nicolai"), [Riemann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernhard_Riemann "Bernhard Riemann"), [Ritter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/August_Ritter_(civil_engineer) "August Ritter (civil engineer)"), [Schering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_Christian_Julius_Schering "Ernst Christian Julius Schering"), [Scherk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Scherk "Heinrich Scherk"), [Schumacher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Christian_Schumacher "Heinrich Christian Schumacher"), [von Staudt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Georg_Christian_von_Staudt "Karl Georg Christian von Staudt"), [Stern](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moritz_Abraham_Stern "Moritz Abraham Stern"), [Ursin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Frederik_Ursin "Georg Frederik Ursin"); as geoscientists [Sartorius von Waltershausen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolfgang_Sartorius_von_Waltershausen "Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen"), and [Wappäus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Eduard_Wapp%C3%A4us "Johann Eduard Wappäus").[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich2000-26)

Gauss did not write any textbook and disliked the [popularization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Popular_science "Popular science") of scientific matters. His only attempts at popularization were his works on the date of Easter (1800/1802) and the essay _Erdmagnetismus und Magnetometer_ of 1836.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Biermann-45) Gauss published his papers and books exclusively in [Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin "Latin") or [German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_language "German language").[[f]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-47)[[g]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-49) He wrote Latin in a classical style but used some customary modifications set by contemporary mathematicians.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200437%E2%80%9338-50)

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Universit%C3%A4ts-Sternwarte_G%C3%B6ttingen_02.jpg/250px-Universit%C3%A4ts-Sternwarte_G%C3%B6ttingen_02.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Universit%C3%A4ts-Sternwarte_G%C3%B6ttingen_02.jpg)

The new Göttingen observatory of 1816; Gauss's living rooms were in the western wing (right)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Die_G%C3%B6ttinger_Sieben_von_Eduard_Ritm%C3%BCller.jpg/250px-Die_G%C3%B6ttinger_Sieben_von_Eduard_Ritm%C3%BCller.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Die_G%C3%B6ttinger_Sieben_von_Eduard_Ritm%C3%BCller.jpg)

[Wilhelm Weber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber "Wilhelm Eduard Weber") and [Heinrich Ewald](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Ewald "Heinrich Ewald") (first row) as members of the [Göttingen Seven](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Seven "Göttingen Seven")

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg/250px-Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed%2C_1855.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gauss_on_his_Deathbed,_1855.jpg)

Gauss on his deathbed (1855) (daguerreotype from Philipp Petri)[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004324-51)

Gauss gave his inaugural lecture at Göttingen University in 1808. He described his approach to astronomy as based on reliable observations and accurate calculations, rather than on belief or empty hypothesizing.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Wittmann-41) At university, he was accompanied by a staff of other lecturers in his disciplines, who completed the educational program; these included the mathematician Thibaut with his lectures,[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-52) the physicist [Mayer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Tobias_Mayer "Johann Tobias Mayer"), known for his textbooks,[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-53) his successor [Weber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber "Wilhelm Eduard Weber") since 1831, and in the observatory [Harding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Ludwig_Harding "Karl Ludwig Harding"), who took the main part of lectures in practical astronomy. When the observatory was completed, Gauss occupied the western wing of the new observatory, while Harding took the eastern.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Beuermann-27) They had once been on friendly terms, but over time they became alienated, possibly – as some biographers presume – because Gauss had wished the equal-ranked Harding to be no more than his assistant or observer.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Beuermann-27)[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-55) Gauss used the new [meridian circles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meridian_circle "Meridian circle") nearly exclusively, and kept them away from Harding, except for some very seldom joint observations.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929106%E2%80%93108-56)

[Brendel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Brendel "Martin Brendel") subdivides Gauss's astronomic activity chronologically into seven periods, of which the years since 1820 are taken as a "period of lower astronomical activity".[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel19297,_128-57) The new, well-equipped observatory did not work as effectively as other ones; Gauss's astronomical research had the character of a one-man enterprise without a long-time observation program, and the university established a place for an assistant only after Harding died in 1834.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-K%C3%BCssner-54)[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929106%E2%80%93108-56)[[i]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-58)

Nevertheless, Gauss twice refused the opportunity to solve the problem, turning down offers from Berlin in 1810 and 1825 to become a full member of the Prussian Academy without burdening lecturing duties, as well as from [Leipzig University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leipzig_University "Leipzig University") in 1810 and from [Vienna University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vienna_University "Vienna University") in 1842, perhaps because of the family's difficult situation.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-K%C3%BCssner-54) Gauss's salary was raised from 1000 [Reichsthaler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reichsthaler "Reichsthaler") in 1810 to 2500 Reichsthaler in 1824,[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Beuermann-27) and in his later years he was one of the best-paid professors of the university.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gerardy-59)

When Gauss was asked for help by his colleague and friend [Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Bessel "Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel") in 1810, who was in trouble at [Königsberg University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K%C3%B6nigsberg_University "Königsberg University") because of his lack of an academic title, Gauss provided a [doctorate _honoris causa_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honorary_degree "Honorary degree") for Bessel from the Philosophy Faculty of Göttingen in March 1811.[[j]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Bessel-62) Gauss gave another recommendation for an honorary degree for [Sophie Germain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sophie_Germain "Sophie Germain") but only shortly before her death, so she never received it.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-63) He also gave successful support to the mathematician [Gotthold Eisenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gotthold_Eisenstein "Gotthold Eisenstein") in Berlin.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-64)

Gauss was loyal to the [House of Hanover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Hanover "House of Hanover"). After King [William IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_IV "William IV") died in 1837, the new Hanoverian King [Ernest Augustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernest_Augustus,_King_of_Hanover "Ernest Augustus, King of Hanover") annulled the 1833 constitution. Seven professors, later known as the "[Göttingen Seven](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Seven "Göttingen Seven")", protested against this, among them his friend and collaborator Wilhelm Weber and Gauss's son-in-law Heinrich Ewald. All of them were dismissed, and three of them were expelled, but Ewald and Weber could stay in Göttingen. Gauss was deeply affected by this quarrel but saw no possibility to help them.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004195%E2%80%93200-65)

Gauss took part in academic administration: three times he was elected as [dean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dean_(education) "Dean (education)") of the Faculty of Philosophy.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004288-66) Being entrusted with the widow's [pension fund](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pension_fund "Pension fund") of the university, he dealt with [actuarial science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Actuarial_science "Actuarial science") and wrote a report on the strategy for stabilizing the benefits. He was appointed director of the Royal Academy of Sciences in Göttingen for nine years.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004288-66)

Gauss remained mentally active into his old age, even while suffering from [gout](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gout "Gout") and general unhappiness. On 23 February 1855, he died of a heart attack in Göttingen;[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200424-15) and was interred in the [Albani Cemetery](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albanifriedhof "Albanifriedhof") there. [Heinrich Ewald](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Ewald "Heinrich Ewald"), Gauss's son-in-law, and [Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolfgang_Sartorius_von_Waltershausen "Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen"), Gauss's close friend and biographer, gave eulogies at his funeral.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen1856104-67)

Gauss was a successful investor and accumulated considerable wealth with stocks and securities, amounting to a value of more than 150,000 Thaler; after his death, about 18,000 Thaler were found hidden in his rooms.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004237-68)

The day after Gauss's death his brain was removed, preserved, and studied by [Rudolf Wagner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rudolf_Wagner "Rudolf Wagner"), who found its mass to be slightly above average, at 1,492 grams (3.29 lb).[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-69)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-70) Wagner's son [Hermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_Wagner_(geographer) "Hermann Wagner (geographer)"), a geographer, estimated the cerebral area to be 219,588 square millimetres (340.362 sq in) in his doctoral thesis.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-71) In 2013, a neurobiologist at the [Max Planck Institute for Biophysical Chemistry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_for_Biophysical_Chemistry "Max Planck Institute for Biophysical Chemistry") in Göttingen discovered that Gauss's brain had been mixed up soon after the first investigations, due to mislabelling, with that of the physician [Conrad Heinrich Fuchs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conrad_Heinrich_Fuchs "Conrad Heinrich Fuchs"), who died in Göttingen a few months after Gauss.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-72) A further investigation showed no remarkable anomalies in the brains of either person. Thus, all investigations of Gauss's brain until 1998, except the first ones of Rudolf and Hermann Wagner, actually refer to the brain of Fuchs.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-73)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Minna_Gau%C3%9F_geb._Waldeck%2C_002.jpg/250px-Minna_Gau%C3%9F_geb._Waldeck%2C_002.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Minna_Gau%C3%9F_geb._Waldeck,_002.jpg)

Gauss's second wife Wilhelmine Waldeck

Gauss married Johanna Osthoff on 9 October 1805 in St. Catherine's church in Brunswick.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200466-74) They had two sons and one daughter: Joseph (1806–1873), Wilhelmina (1808–1840), and Louis (1809–1810). Johanna died on 11 October 1809, one month after the birth of Louis, who himself died a few months later.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu%C3%9Fing198244-75) Gauss chose the first names of his children in honour of [Giuseppe Piazzi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giuseppe_Piazzi "Giuseppe Piazzi"), Wilhelm Olbers, and Karl Ludwig Harding, the discoverers of the first asteroids.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200477,_88,_93-76)

On 4 August 1810, Gauss married Wilhelmine (Minna) Waldeck, a friend of his first wife, with whom he had three more children: Eugen (later Eugene) (1811–1896), Wilhelm (later William) (1813–1879), and Therese (1816–1864). Minna Gauss died on 12 September 1831 after being seriously ill for more than a decade.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-77) Therese then took over the household and cared for Gauss for the rest of his life; after her father's death, she married actor Constantin Staufenau.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004374-78) Her sister Wilhelmina married the orientalist [Heinrich Ewald](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Ewald "Heinrich Ewald").[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004206-79) Gauss's mother Dorothea lived in his house from 1817 until she died in 1839.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-scientificmonthly-14)

The eldest son Joseph, while still a schoolboy, helped his father as an assistant during the survey campaign in the summer of 1821. After a short time at university, in 1824 Joseph joined the [Hanoverian army](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanoverian_army "Hanoverian army") and assisted in surveying again in 1829. In the 1830s he was responsible for the enlargement of the survey network into the western parts of the kingdom. With his geodetical qualifications, he left the service and engaged in the construction of the railway network as director of the [Royal Hanoverian State Railways](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Hanoverian_State_Railways "Royal Hanoverian State Railways"). In 1836 he studied the railroad system in the US for some months.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gerardy-59)[[k]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-82)

Eugen left Göttingen in September 1830 and emigrated to the United States, where he spent five years with the army. He then worked for the [American Fur Company](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Fur_Company "American Fur Company") in the Midwest. He later moved to [Missouri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Missouri "Missouri") and became a successful businessman.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gerardy-59) Wilhelm married a niece of the astronomer [Bessel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel "Friedrich Bessel");[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-83) he then moved to Missouri, started as a farmer and became wealthy in the shoe business in [St. Louis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri "St. Louis, Missouri") in later years.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-84) Eugene and William have numerous descendants in America, but the Gauss descendants left in Germany all derive from Joseph, as the daughters had no children.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gerardy-59)

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_Karikatur_von_Abraham_Gotthelf_K%C3%A4stner%2C_1795.jpg/250px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_Karikatur_von_Abraham_Gotthelf_K%C3%A4stner%2C_1795.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_Karikatur_von_Abraham_Gotthelf_K%C3%A4stner,_1795.jpg)

A student draws his professor of mathematics: Caricature of [Abraham Gotthelf Kästner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham_Gotthelf_K%C3%A4stner "Abraham Gotthelf Kästner") by Gauss (1795)[[l]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-85)

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_003.jpg/250px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_003.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_003.jpg)

A student draws his professor of mathematics: Gauss sketched by his student [Johann Benedict Listing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Benedict_Listing "Johann Benedict Listing"), 1830

In the first two decades of the 19th century, Gauss was the only important mathematician in Germany comparable to the leading French mathematicians.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Schubring-86) His _Disquisitiones Arithmeticae_ was the first mathematical book from Germany to be translated into the French language.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-87)

Gauss was "in front of the new development" with documented research since 1799, his wealth of new ideas, and his rigour of demonstration.[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein1894100%E2%80%93101-88) In contrast to previous mathematicians like [Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler"), who let their readers take part in their reasoning, including certain erroneous deviations from the correct path,[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein19795%E2%80%936-89) Gauss introduced a new style of direct and complete exposition that did not attempt to show the reader the author's train of thought.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004217-90)

> Gauss was the first to restore that _rigor_ of demonstration which we admire in the ancients and which had been forced unduly into the background by the exclusive interest of the preceding period in _new_ developments.

— [Klein 1894](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#CITEREFKlein1894), p.101

But for himself, he propagated a quite different ideal, given in a letter to Farkas Bolyai as follows:[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-91)

> It is not knowledge, but the act of learning, not possession but the act of getting there, which grants the greatest enjoyment. When I have clarified and exhausted a subject, then I turn away from it, in order to go into darkness again.

— [Dunnington 2004](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#CITEREFDunnington2004), p.416

His posthumous papers, his scientific [diary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_diary "Gauss's diary"),[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-92) and short glosses in his own textbooks show that he empirically worked to a great extent.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann19224%E2%80%936-93)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger193318-94) He was a lifelong busy and enthusiastic calculator, working extraordinarily quickly and checking his results through estimation. Nevertheless, his calculations were not always free from mistakes.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaennchen193064%E2%80%9365-95) He coped with the enormous workload by using skillful tools.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaennchen19304%E2%80%939-96) Gauss used numerous [mathematical tables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_table "Mathematical table"), examined their exactness, and constructed new tables on various matters for personal use.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-97) He developed new tools for effective calculation, for example the [Gaussian elimination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_elimination "Gaussian elimination").[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-98) Gauss's calculations and the tables he prepared were often more precise than practically necessary.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaennchen19303-99) Very likely, this method gave him additional material for his theoretical work.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaennchen19304%E2%80%939-96)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann19225-100)

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/GaussSiegel1777-1855.png/250px-GaussSiegel1777-1855.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:GaussSiegel1777-1855.png)

Gauss's [seal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seal_(emblem) "Seal (emblem)") with his motto _Pauca sed Matura_

Gauss was only willing to publish work when he considered it complete and above criticism. This [perfectionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perfectionism_(psychology) "Perfectionism (psychology)") was in keeping with the motto of his personal [seal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seal_(emblem) "Seal (emblem)")_Pauca sed Matura_ ("Few, but Ripe"). Many colleagues encouraged him to publicize new ideas and sometimes rebuked him if he hesitated too long, in their opinion. Gauss defended himself by claiming that the initial discovery of ideas was easy, but preparing a presentable elaboration was a demanding matter for him, for either lack of time or "serenity of mind".[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Biermann-45) Nevertheless, he published many short communications of urgent content in various journals, but left a considerable literary estate, too.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197929-101)[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004420%E2%80%93430-102) Gauss referred to mathematics as "the queen of sciences" and arithmetics as "the queen of mathematics",[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185679-103) and supposedly once espoused a belief in the necessity of immediately understanding [Euler's identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_identity "Euler's identity") as a benchmark pursuant to becoming a first-class mathematician.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-First-Class-104)

On certain occasions, Gauss claimed that the ideas of another scholar had already been in his possession previously. Thus his concept of priority as "the first to discover, not the first to publish" differed from that of his scientific contemporaries.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stigler-105) In contrast to his perfectionism in presenting mathematical ideas, his citations were criticized as negligent. He justified himself with an unusual view of correct citation practice: he would only give complete references, with respect to the previous authors of importance, which no one should ignore, but citing in this way would require knowledge of the history of science and more time than he wished to spend.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Biermann-45)

Soon after Gauss's death, his friend Sartorius published the first biography (1856), written in a rather enthusiastic style. Sartorius saw him as a serene and forward-striving man with childlike modesty,[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen1856102-106) but also of "iron character"[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185695-107) with an unshakeable strength of mind.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen18568-108) Apart from his closer circle, others regarded him as reserved and unapproachable "like an [Olympian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Twelve_Olympians "Twelve Olympians") sitting enthroned on the summit of science".[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu%C3%9Fing198241-109) His close contemporaries agreed that Gauss was a man of difficult character. He often refused to accept compliments. His visitors were occasionally irritated by his grumpy behaviour, but a short time later his mood could change, and he would become a charming, open-minded host.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Biermann-45) Gauss disliked polemic natures; together with his colleague [Hausmann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Ludwig_Hausmann "Johann Friedrich Ludwig Hausmann") he opposed to a call for [Justus Liebig](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Justus_Liebig "Justus Liebig") on a university chair in Göttingen, "because he was always involved in some polemic."[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004253-110)

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/G%C3%B6ttingen%2C_Kurze_Stra%C3%9Fe_15%2C_001.jpg/250px-G%C3%B6ttingen%2C_Kurze_Stra%C3%9Fe_15%2C_001.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:G%C3%B6ttingen,_Kurze_Stra%C3%9Fe_15,_001.jpg)

Gauss's residence from 1808 to 1816 in the first floor

Gauss's life was overshadowed by severe problems in his family. When his first wife Johanna suddenly died shortly after the birth of their third child, he revealed the grief in a last letter to his dead wife in the style of an ancient [threnody](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Threnody "Threnody"), the most personal of his surviving documents.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-111)[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200494%E2%80%9395-112) His second wife and his two daughters suffered from [tuberculosis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tuberculosis "Tuberculosis").[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004206,_374-113) In a letter to [Bessel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Bessel "Friedrich Wilhelm Bessel"), dated December 1831, Gauss hinted at his distress, describing himself as "the victim of the worst domestic sufferings".[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Biermann-45)

Because of his wife's illness, both younger sons were educated for some years in [Celle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celle "Celle"), far from Göttingen. The military career of his elder son Joseph ended after more than two decades at the poorly paid rank of [first lieutenant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_lieutenant "First lieutenant"), although he had acquired a considerable knowledge of geodesy. He needed financial support from his father even after he was married.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gerardy-59) The second son Eugen shared a good measure of his father's talent in computation and languages but had a lively and sometimes rebellious character. He wanted to study philology, whereas Gauss wanted him to become a lawyer. Having run up debts and caused a scandal in public,[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-gausschildren-114) Eugen suddenly left Göttingen under dramatic circumstances in September 1830 and emigrated via Bremen to the United States. He wasted the little money he had taken to start, after which his father refused further financial support.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gerardy-59) The youngest son Wilhelm wanted to qualify for agricultural administration, but had difficulties getting an appropriate education, and eventually emigrated as well. Only Gauss's youngest daughter Therese accompanied him in his last years of life.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004374-78)

In his later years Gauss habitually collected various types of useful or useless numerical data, such as the number of paths from his home to certain places in Göttingen or peoples' ages in days; he congratulated [Humboldt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt "Alexander von Humboldt") in December 1851 for having reached the same age as [Isaac Newton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Newton "Isaac Newton") at his death, calculated in days.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185671-115)

Beyond his excellent knowledge of [Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin "Latin"), he was also acquainted with modern languages. Gauss read both classical and modern literature, and English and French works in the original languages.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004241-116)[[m]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-118) His favorite English author was [Walter Scott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Scott "Walter Scott"), his favorite German [Jean Paul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_Paul "Jean Paul"). At the age of 62, he began to teach himself [Russian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_language "Russian language"), very likely to understand scientific writings from Russia, among them those of [Lobachevsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolai_Lobachevsky "Nikolai Lobachevsky") on non-Euclidean geometry.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-119)[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu%C3%9Fing198280-120) Gauss liked singing and went to concerts.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu%C3%9Fing198281-121) He was a busy newspaper reader; in his last years, he would visit an academic press salon of the university every noon.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185694-122) Gauss did not care much for philosophy, and mocked the "splitting hairs of the so-called metaphysicians", by which he meant proponents of the contemporary school of _[Naturphilosophie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Naturphilosophie "Naturphilosophie")_.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu%C3%9Fing198279-123)

Gauss had an "aristocratic and through and through conservative nature", with little respect for people's intelligence and morals, following the motto "[mundus vult decipi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mundus_vult_decipi,_ergo_decipiatur "Mundus vult decipi, ergo decipiatur")".[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185694-122) He disliked Napoleon and his system and was horrified by violence and revolution of all kinds. Thus he condemned the methods of the [Revolutions of 1848](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848 "Revolutions of 1848"), though he agreed with some of their aims, such as that of a unified Germany.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185695-107)[[n]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-124) He had a low estimation of the constitutional system and he criticized parliamentarians of his time for their perceived ignorance and logical errors.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185694-122)

Some Gauss biographers have speculated on his religious beliefs. He sometimes said "God arithmetizes"[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen185697-125) and "I succeeded – not on account of my hard efforts, but by the grace of the Lord."[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-126) Gauss was a member of the [Lutheran church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lutheran_church "Lutheran church"), like most of the population in northern Germany, but it seems that he did not believe all Lutheran [dogma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dogma "Dogma") or understand the Bible fully literally.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004300-127) According to Sartorius, Gauss' [religious tolerance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Religious_tolerance "Religious tolerance"), "insatiable thirst for truth" and sense of justice were motivated by his religious convictions.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESartorius_von_Waltershausen1856100-128)

### Algebra and number theory

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Algebra and number theory")]

#### Fundamental theorem of algebra

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Fundamental theorem of algebra")]

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/DBP_1977_928_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F.jpg/250px-DBP_1977_928_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:DBP_1977_928_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F.jpg)

German stamp commemorating Gauss's 200th anniversary: the [complex plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_plane "Complex plane") or _Gauss plane_

In his doctoral thesis from 1799, Gauss proved the [fundamental theorem of algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra "Fundamental theorem of algebra") which states that every non-constant single-variable [polynomial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polynomial "Polynomial") with complex coefficients has at least one complex [root](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Root_of_a_function "Root of a function"). Mathematicians including [Jean le Rond d'Alembert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jean_le_Rond_d%27Alembert "Jean le Rond d'Alembert") had produced false proofs before him, and Gauss's dissertation contains a critique of d'Alembert's work. He subsequently produced three other proofs, the last one in 1849 being generally rigorous. His attempts led to considerable clarification of the concept of complex numbers.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-129)

#### _Disquisitiones Arithmeticae_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Disquisitiones Arithmeticae")]

In the preface to the _Disquisitiones_, Gauss dates the beginning of his work on number theory to 1795. By studying the works of previous mathematicians like Fermat, Euler, Lagrange, and Legendre, he realized that these scholars had already found much of what he had independently discovered.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann19228-130) The _[Disquisitiones Arithmeticae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disquisitiones\_Arithmeticae "Disquisitiones Arithmeticae")_, written in 1798 and published in 1801, consolidated number theory as a discipline and covered both elementary and algebraic [number theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number_theory "Number theory"). Therein he introduces the [triple bar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triple_bar "Triple bar") symbol (≡) for [congruence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congruence_relation "Congruence relation") and uses it for a clean presentation of [modular arithmetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_arithmetic "Modular arithmetic").[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann19228%E2%80%939-131) It deals with the [unique factorization theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unique_factorization_theorem "Unique factorization theorem") and [primitive roots modulo n](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primitive_root_modulo_n "Primitive root modulo n"). In the main sections, Gauss presents the first two proofs of the law of [quadratic reciprocity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_reciprocity "Quadratic reciprocity")[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192216%E2%80%9325-132) and develops the theories of [binary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_quadratic_form "Binary quadratic form")[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192214%E2%80%9316,_25-133) and ternary [quadratic forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadratic_form "Quadratic form").[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192225%E2%80%9328-134)

The _Disquisitiones_ include the [Gauss composition law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_composition_law "Gauss composition law") for binary quadratic forms, as well as the enumeration of the number of representations of an integer as the sum of three squares. As an almost immediate corollary of his [theorem on three squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legendre%27s_three-square_theorem "Legendre's three-square theorem"), he proves the triangular case of the [Fermat polygonal number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_polygonal_number_theorem "Fermat polygonal number theorem") for _n_ = 3.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192229-135) From several analytic results on [class numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_class_group "Ideal class group") that Gauss gives without proof towards the end of the fifth section,[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192222%E2%80%9323-136) it appears that Gauss already knew the [class number formula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_number_formula "Class number formula") in 1801.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192266%E2%80%9369-137)

In the last section, Gauss gives proof for the [constructibility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constructible_polygon "Constructible polygon") of a regular [heptadecagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heptadecagon "Heptadecagon") (17-sided polygon) with [straightedge and compass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compass_and_straightedge_constructions "Compass and straightedge constructions") by reducing this geometrical problem to an algebraic one.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Denker-138) He shows that a regular polygon is constructible if the number of its sides is either a [power of 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_of_2 "Power of 2") or the product of a power of 2 and any number of distinct [Fermat primes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat_prime "Fermat prime"). In the same section, he gives a result on the number of solutions of certain cubic polynomials with coefficients in [finite fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Finite_field "Finite field"), which amounts to counting integral points on an [elliptic curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_curve "Elliptic curve").[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stuhler-139) An unfinished chapter, consisting of work done during 1797–1799, was found among his papers after his death.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200444-140)[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Frei-141)

#### Further investigations

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Further investigations")]

One of Gauss's first results was the empirically found conjecture of 1792 – the later called [prime number theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_number_theorem "Prime number theorem") – giving an estimation of the number of prime numbers by using the [integral logarithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithmic_integral_function "Logarithmic integral function").[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-142)[[o]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-144)

In 1816, [Olbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Matthias_Olbers "Heinrich Wilhelm Matthias Olbers") encouraged Gauss to compete for a prize from the French Academy for a proof for [Fermat's Last Theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_Last_Theorem "Fermat's Last Theorem"); he refused, considering the topic uninteresting. However, after his death a short undated paper was found with proofs of the theorem for the cases _n_ = 3 and _n_ = 5.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-145) The particular case of _n_ = 3 was proved much earlier by [Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler"), but Gauss developed a more streamlined proof which made use of [Eisenstein integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eisenstein_integers "Eisenstein integers"); though more general, the proof was simpler than in the real integers case.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192260%E2%80%9361-146)

Gauss contributed to solving the [Kepler conjecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler_conjecture "Kepler conjecture") in 1831 with the proof that a [greatest packing density](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Close-packing_of_equal_spheres "Close-packing of equal spheres") of spheres in the three-dimensional space is given when the centres of the spheres form a [cubic face-centred](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system "Cubic crystal system") arrangement,[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-147) when he reviewed a book of [Ludwig August Seeber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_August_Seeber "Ludwig August Seeber") on the theory of reduction of positive ternary quadratic forms.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-148) Having noticed some lacks in Seeber's proof, he simplified many of his arguments, proved the central conjecture, and remarked that this theorem is equivalent to the Kepler conjecture for regular arrangements.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-149)

In two papers on [biquadratic residues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quartic_reciprocity "Quartic reciprocity") (1828, 1832) Gauss introduced the [ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_theory "Ring theory") of [Gaussian integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_integers "Gaussian integers")![Image 25: {\displaystyle \mathbb {Z} [i]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9ffa94e9e2e6d9e5e5373d5fafb954b902743fde), showed that it is a [unique factorization domain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unique_factorization_domain "Unique factorization domain"),[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Kleiner1998-150) and generalized some key arithmetic concepts, such as [Fermat's little theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermat%27s_little_theorem "Fermat's little theorem") and [Gauss's lemma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_lemma_(number_theory) "Gauss's lemma (number theory)"). The main objective of introducing this ring was to formulate the law of biquadratic reciprocity[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Kleiner1998-150) – as Gauss discovered, rings of complex integers are the natural setting for such higher reciprocity laws.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-151)

In the second paper, he stated the general law of biquadratic reciprocity and proved several special cases of it. In an earlier publication from 1818 containing his fifth and sixth proofs of quadratic reciprocity, he claimed the techniques of these proofs ([Gauss sums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_sum "Gauss sum")) can be applied to prove higher reciprocity laws.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann192252,_57%E2%80%9359-152)

One of Gauss's first discoveries was the notion of the [arithmetic-geometric mean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic-geometric_mean "Arithmetic-geometric mean") (AGM) of two positive real numbers.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger193341%E2%80%9357-153) He discovered its relation to elliptic integrals in the years 1798–1799 through [Landen's transformation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landen%27s_transformation "Landen's transformation"), and a diary entry recorded the discovery of the connection of [Gauss's constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_constant "Gauss's constant") to [lemniscatic elliptic functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lemniscatic_elliptic_functions "Lemniscatic elliptic functions"), a result that Gauss stated "will surely open an entirely new field of analysis".[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Cox-154) He also made early inroads into the more formal issues of the foundations of [complex analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_analysis "Complex analysis"), and from a letter to Bessel in 1811 it is clear that he knew the "fundamental theorem of complex analysis" – [Cauchy's integral theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cauchy%27s_integral_theorem "Cauchy's integral theorem") – and understood the notion of [complex residues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Residue_(complex_analysis) "Residue (complex analysis)") when integrating around [poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pole_(complex_analysis) "Pole (complex analysis)").[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stuhler-139)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-155)

[Euler's pentagonal numbers theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentagonal_number_theorem "Pentagonal number theorem"), together with other researches on the AGM and lemniscatic functions, led him to plenty of results on [Jacobi theta functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobi_theta_functions "Jacobi theta functions"),[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stuhler-139) culminating in the discovery in 1808 of the later called [Jacobi triple product identity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacobi_triple_product_identity "Jacobi triple product identity"), which includes Euler's theorem as a special case.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-156) His works show that he knew modular transformations of order 3, 5, 7 for elliptic functions since 1808.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933185%E2%80%93186-157)[[p]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-158)[[q]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-160)

Several mathematical fragments in his [Nachlass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nachlass "Nachlass") indicate that he knew parts of the modern theory of [modular forms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_forms "Modular forms").[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stuhler-139) In his work on the [multivalued](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multivalued_function "Multivalued function") AGM of two complex numbers, he discovered a deep connection between the infinitely many values of the AGM and its two "simplest values".[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Cox-154) In his unpublished writings he recognized and made a sketch of the key concept of [fundamental domain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_domain "Fundamental domain") for the [modular group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modular_group "Modular group").[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933101%E2%80%93106-161)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Houzel-162) One of Gauss's sketches of this kind was a drawing of a [tessellation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tessellation "Tessellation") of the [unit disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_disk "Unit disk") by "equilateral" [hyperbolic triangles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbolic_triangle "Hyperbolic triangle") with all angles equal to ![Image 26: {\displaystyle \pi /4}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1707aa0fec2c8ef008b9e30b6045fbf95dab9e20).[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-163)

An example of Gauss's insight in analysis is the cryptic remark that the principles of circle division by compass and straightedge can also be applied to the division of the [lemniscate curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lemniscate_curve "Lemniscate curve"), which inspired Abel's theorem on lemniscate division.[[r]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-164) Another example is his publication "Summatio quarundam serierum singularium" (1811) on the determination of the sign of [quadratic Gauss sums](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quadratic_Gauss_sums&action=edit&redlink=1 "Quadratic Gauss sums (page does not exist)"), in which he solved the main problem by introducing [q-analogs of binomial coefficients](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_binomial_coefficient "Gaussian binomial coefficient") and manipulating them by several original identities that seem to stem from his work on elliptic function theory; however, Gauss cast his argument in a formal way that does not reveal its origin in elliptic function theory, and only the later work of mathematicians such as [Jacobi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Gustav_Jacob_Jacobi "Carl Gustav Jacob Jacobi") and [Hermite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermite "Hermite") has exposed the crux of his argument.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933122%E2%80%93123-165)

In the "Disquisitiones generales circa series infinitam..." (1813), he provides the first systematic treatment of the general [hypergeometric function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypergeometric_function "Hypergeometric function")![Image 27: {\displaystyle F(\alpha ,\beta ,\gamma ,x)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f479aea7571b5ea41a92cbfa38a8f51826ea8471), and shows that many of the functions known at the time are special cases of the hypergeometric function.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933136%E2%80%93142-166) This work is the first exact inquiry into [convergence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convergent_series "Convergent series") of infinite series in the history of mathematics.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933142-167) Furthermore, it deals with infinite [continued fractions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continued_fraction "Continued fraction") arising as ratios of hypergeometric functions, which are now called [Gauss continued fractions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_continued_fraction "Gauss continued fraction").[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933136%E2%80%93154-168)

In 1823, Gauss won the prize of the Danish Society with an essay on [conformal mappings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conformal_mapping "Conformal mapping"), which contains several developments that pertain to the field of complex analysis.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191790%E2%80%9391-169) Gauss stated that angle-preserving mappings in the complex plane must be complex [analytic functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Analytic_function "Analytic function"), and used the later-named [Beltrami equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beltrami_equation "Beltrami equation") to prove the existence of [isothermal coordinates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isothermal_coordinates "Isothermal coordinates") on analytic surfaces. The essay concludes with examples of conformal mappings into a sphere and an [ellipsoid of revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipsoid_of_revolution "Ellipsoid of revolution").[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEB%C3%BChler1981103-170)

Gauss often deduced theorems [inductively](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductive_reasoning "Inductive reasoning") from numerical data he had collected empirically.[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger193318-94) As such, the use of efficient algorithms to facilitate calculations was vital to his research, and he made many contributions to [numerical analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numerical_analysis "Numerical analysis"), such as the method of [Gaussian quadrature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_quadrature "Gaussian quadrature"), published in 1816.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-171)

In a private letter to [Gerling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christian_Ludwig_Gerling "Christian Ludwig Gerling") from 1823,[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-172) he described a solution of a 4x4 system of linear equations with the [Gauss-Seidel method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss-Seidel_method "Gauss-Seidel method") – an "indirect" [iterative method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iterative_method "Iterative method") for the solution of linear systems, and recommended it over the usual method of "direct elimination" for systems of more than two equations.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-173)

Gauss invented an algorithm for calculating what is now called [discrete Fourier transforms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Discrete_Fourier_transform "Discrete Fourier transform") when calculating the orbits of Pallas and Juno in 1805, 160 years before [Cooley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Cooley "James Cooley") and [Tukey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Tukey "John Tukey") found their similar [Cooley–Tukey algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooley-Tukey_FFT_algorithm "Cooley-Tukey FFT algorithm").[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-174) He developed it as a [trigonometric interpolation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trigonometric_interpolation "Trigonometric interpolation") method, but the paper _Theoria Interpolationis Methodo Nova Tractata_ was published only posthumously in 1876,[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-175) well after [Joseph Fourier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Fourier "Joseph Fourier")'s introduction of the subject in 1807.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-176)

#### Differential geometry

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Differential geometry")]

The geodetic survey of [Hanover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanover "Hanover") fuelled Gauss's interest in [differential geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_geometry "Differential geometry") and [topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology "Topology"), fields of mathematics dealing with [curves](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Curve "Curve") and [surfaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_(topology) "Surface (topology)"). This led him in 1828 to the publication of a work that marks the birth of modern [differential geometry of surfaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_geometry_of_surfaces "Differential geometry of surfaces"), as it departed from the traditional ways of treating surfaces as [cartesian graphs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system "Cartesian coordinate system") of functions of two variables, and that initiated the exploration of surfaces from the "inner" point of view of a two-dimensional being constrained to move on it. As a result, the [Theorema Egregium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theorema_Egregium "Theorema Egregium") (_remarkable theorem_), established a property of the notion of [Gaussian curvature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_curvature "Gaussian curvature").
