Informally, the theorem says that the curvature of a surface can be determined entirely by measuring [angles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle "Angle") and [distances](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distance "Distance") on the surface, regardless of the [embedding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embedding "Embedding") of the surface in three-dimensional or two-dimensional space.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel1917110%E2%80%93119-177)

The Theorema Egregium leads to the abstraction of surfaces as doubly-extended [manifolds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manifold "Manifold"); it clarifies the distinction between the intrinsic properties of the manifold (the [metric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_tensor "Metric tensor")) and its physical realization in ambient space. A consequence is the impossibility of an isometric transformation between surfaces of different Gaussian curvature. This means practically that a [sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere "Sphere") or an [ellipsoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipsoid "Ellipsoid") cannot be transformed to a plane without distortion, which causes a fundamental problem in designing [projections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Map_projection "Map projection") for geographical maps.[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel1917110%E2%80%93119-177) A portion of this essay is dedicated to a profound study of [geodesics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesic "Geodesic"). In particular, Gauss proves the local [Gauss–Bonnet theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Bonnet_theorem "Gauss–Bonnet theorem") on geodesic triangles, and generalizes [Legendre's theorem on spherical triangles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legendre%27s_theorem_on_spherical_triangles "Legendre's theorem on spherical triangles") to geodesic triangles on arbitrary surfaces with continuous curvature; he found that the angles of a "sufficiently small" geodesic triangle deviate from that of a planar triangle of the same sides in a way that depends only on the values of the surface curvature at the vertices of the triangle, regardless of the behaviour of the surface in the triangle interior.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel1917105%E2%80%93106-178)

Gauss's memoir from 1828 lacks the conception of [geodesic curvature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesic_curvature "Geodesic curvature"). However, in a previously unpublished manuscript, very likely written in 1822–1825, he introduced the term "side curvature" (German: "Seitenkrümmung") and proved its [invariance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Invariant_(mathematics) "Invariant (mathematics)") under isometric transformations, a result that was later obtained by [Ferdinand Minding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_Minding "Ferdinand Minding") and published by him in 1830. This Gauss paper contains the core of his lemma on total curvature, but also its generalization, found and proved by [Pierre Ossian Bonnet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_Ossian_Bonnet "Pierre Ossian Bonnet") in 1848 and known as the [Gauss–Bonnet theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Bonnet_theorem "Gauss–Bonnet theorem").[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBolza192170%E2%80%9374-179)

#### Non-Euclidean geometry

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Non-Euclidean geometry")]

[![Image 28](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg/250px-Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_1828.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bendixen_-_Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_1828.jpg)

Lithograph of Gauss by [Siegfried Bendixen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siegfried_Bendixen "Siegfried Bendixen") (1828)

During Gauss' lifetime, the [Parallel postulate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallel_postulate "Parallel postulate") of [Euclidean geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_geometry "Euclidean geometry") was heavily discussed.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191719%E2%80%9320-180) Numerous efforts were made to prove it in the frame of the Euclidean [axioms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom "Axiom"), whereas some mathematicians discussed the possibility of geometrical systems without it.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEB%C3%BChler1981100%E2%80%93102-181) Gauss thought about the basics of geometry from the 1790s on, but only realized in the 1810s that a non-Euclidean geometry without the parallel postulate could solve the problem.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197957%E2%80%9360-182)[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191719%E2%80%9320-180) In a letter to [Franz Taurinus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Taurinus "Franz Taurinus") of 1824, he presented a short comprehensible outline of what he named a "[non-Euclidean geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry "Non-Euclidean geometry")",[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-:0-183) but he strongly forbade Taurinus to make any use of it.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197957%E2%80%9360-182) Gauss is credited with having been the one to first discover and study non-Euclidean geometry, even coining the term as well.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-184)[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-:0-183)[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-185)

The first publications on non-Euclidean geometry in the history of mathematics were authored by [Nikolai Lobachevsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nikolai_Lobachevsky "Nikolai Lobachevsky") in 1829 and [Janos Bolyai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Janos_Bolyai "Janos Bolyai") in 1832.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEB%C3%BChler1981100%E2%80%93102-181) In the following years, Gauss wrote his ideas on the topic but did not publish them, thus avoiding influencing the contemporary scientific discussion.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197957%E2%80%9360-182)[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-186) Gauss commended the ideas of Janos Bolyai in a letter to his father and university friend Farkas Bolyai[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-187) claiming that these were congruent to his own thoughts of some decades.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197957%E2%80%9360-182)[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-188) However, it is not quite clear to what extent he preceded Lobachevsky and Bolyai, as his written remarks are vague and obscure.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEB%C3%BChler1981100%E2%80%93102-181)

[Sartorius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolfgang_Sartorius_von_Waltershausen "Wolfgang Sartorius von Waltershausen") first mentioned Gauss's work on non-Euclidean geometry in 1856, but only the publication of Gauss's [Nachlass](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nachlass "Nachlass") in Volume VIII of the Collected Works (1900) showed Gauss's ideas on the matter, at a time when non-Euclidean geometry was still an object of some controversy.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197957%E2%80%9360-182)

Gauss was also an early pioneer of [topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology "Topology") or _Geometria Situs_, as it was called in his lifetime. The first proof of the [fundamental theorem of algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_theorem_of_algebra "Fundamental theorem of algebra") in 1799 contained an essentially topological argument; fifty years later, he further developed the topological argument in his fourth proof of this theorem.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOstrowski19201%E2%80%9318-189)

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_B%C3%BCste_von_Heinrich_Hesemann%2C_1855.jpg/250px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_B%C3%BCste_von_Heinrich_Hesemann%2C_1855.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_B%C3%BCste_von_Heinrich_Hesemann,_1855.jpg)

Gauss bust by Heinrich Hesemann (1855)[[s]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-190)

Another encounter with topological notions occurred to him in the course of his astronomical work in 1804, when he determined the limits of the region on the [celestial sphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_sphere "Celestial sphere") in which comets and asteroids might appear, and which he termed "Zodiacus". He discovered that if the Earth's and comet's orbits are [linked](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Link_(knot_theory) "Link (knot theory)"), then by topological reasons the Zodiacus is the entire sphere. In 1848, in the context of the discovery of the asteroid [7 Iris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/7_Iris "7 Iris"), he published a further qualitative discussion of the Zodiacus.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Epple_1998_45%E2%80%9352-191)

In Gauss's letters of 1820–1830, he thought intensively on topics with close affinity to Geometria Situs, and became gradually conscious of semantic difficulty in this field. Fragments from this period reveal that he tried to classify "tract figures", which are closed plane curves with a finite number of transverse self-intersections, that may also be planar projections of [knots](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knot_theory "Knot theory").[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-192) To do so he devised a symbolical scheme, the [Gauss code](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_code "Gauss code"), that in a sense captured the characteristic features of tract figures.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-193)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191750%E2%80%9351-194)

In a fragment from 1833, Gauss defined the [linking number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linking_number "Linking number") of two space curves by a certain double integral, and in doing so provided for the first time an analytical formulation of a topological phenomenon. On the same note, he lamented the little progress made in Geometria Situs, and remarked that one of its central problems will be "to count the intertwinings of two closed or infinite curves". His notebooks from that period reveal that he was also thinking about other topological objects such as [braids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braid_theory "Braid theory") and [tangles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangle_(mathematics) "Tangle (mathematics)").[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Epple_1998_45%E2%80%9352-191)

Gauss's influence in later years to the emerging field of topology, which he held in high esteem, was through occasional remarks and oral communications to Mobius and Listing.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191751%E2%80%9355-195)

### Minor mathematical accomplishments

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Minor mathematical accomplishments")]

Gauss applied the concept of complex numbers to solve well-known problems in a new concise way. For example, in a short note from 1836 on geometric aspects of the ternary forms and their application to crystallography,[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-196) he stated the [fundamental theorem of axonometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axonometry "Axonometry"), which tells how to represent a 3D cube on a 2D plane with complete accuracy, via complex numbers.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-197) He described rotations of this sphere as the action of certain [linear fractional transformations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mobius_transformation "Mobius transformation") on the extended complex plane,[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933198-198) and gave a proof for the geometric theorem that the [altitudes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altitude_(triangle) "Altitude (triangle)") of a triangle always meet in a single [orthocenter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthocenter "Orthocenter").[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-199)

Gauss was concerned with [John Napier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Napier "John Napier")'s "[Pentagramma mirificum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentagramma_mirificum "Pentagramma mirificum")" – a certain spherical [pentagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentagram "Pentagram") – for several decades;[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-200) he approached it from various points of view, and gradually gained a full understanding of its geometric, algebraic, and analytic aspects.[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-201) In particular, in 1843 he stated and proved several theorems connecting elliptic functions, Napier spherical pentagons, and Poncelet pentagons in the plane.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-202)

Furthermore, he contributed a solution to the problem of constructing the largest-area ellipse inside a given [quadrilateral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadrilateral "Quadrilateral"),[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-203)[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191771-72-204) and discovered a surprising result about the computation of area of [pentagons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentagon "Pentagon").[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-205)[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESt%C3%A4ckel191776-206)

[![Image 30](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_Pastellgem%C3%A4lde_von_Johann_Christian_August_Schwartz%2C_1803%2C_ohne_Rahmen.jpg/250px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_Pastellgem%C3%A4lde_von_Johann_Christian_August_Schwartz%2C_1803%2C_ohne_Rahmen.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_Pastellgem%C3%A4lde_von_Johann_Christian_August_Schwartz,_1803,_ohne_Rahmen.jpg)

Carl Friedrich Gauss 1803 by Johann Christian August Schwartz

On 1 January 1801, Italian astronomer [Giuseppe Piazzi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giuseppe_Piazzi "Giuseppe Piazzi") discovered a new celestial object, presumed it to be the long searched planet between Mars and Jupiter according to the so-called [Titius–Bode law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titius%E2%80%93Bode_law "Titius–Bode law"), and named it [Ceres](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet) "Ceres (dwarf planet)").[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-207) He could track it only for a short time until it disappeared behind the glare of the Sun. The mathematical tools of the time were not sufficient to predict the location of its reappearance from the few data available. Gauss tackled the problem and predicted a position for possible rediscovery in December 1801. This turned out to be accurate within a half-degree when [Franz Xaver von Zach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Xaver_von_Zach "Franz Xaver von Zach") on 7 and 31 December at [Gotha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gotha_Observatory "Gotha Observatory"), and independently [Heinrich Olbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Wilhelm_Matth%C3%A4us_Olbers "Heinrich Wilhelm Matthäus Olbers") on 1 and 2 January in [Bremen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bremen "Bremen"), identified the object near the predicted position.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-208)[[t]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-209)

[Gauss's method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_method "Gauss's method") leads to an equation of the eighth degree, of which one solution, the Earth's orbit, is known. The solution sought is then separated from the remaining six based on physical conditions. In this work, Gauss used comprehensive approximation methods which he created for that purpose.[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein19798-210)

The discovery of Ceres led Gauss to the theory of the motion of planetoids disturbed by large planets, eventually published in 1809 as _Theoria motus corporum coelestium in sectionibus conicis solem ambientum_.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-211) It introduced the [Gaussian gravitational constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_gravitational_constant "Gaussian gravitational constant").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Wittmann-41)

Since the new asteroids had been discovered, Gauss occupied himself with the [perturbations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy) "Perturbation (astronomy)") of their [orbital elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_elements "Orbital elements"). Firstly he examined Ceres with analytical methods similar to those of Laplace, but his favorite object was [Pallas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/(2)_Pallas "(2) Pallas"), because of its great [eccentricity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eccentricity_(astronomy) "Eccentricity (astronomy)") and [orbital inclination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_inclination "Orbital inclination"), whereby Laplace's method did not work. Gauss used his own tools: the [arithmetic–geometric mean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arithmetic%E2%80%93geometric_mean "Arithmetic–geometric mean"), the [hypergeometric function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypergeometric_function "Hypergeometric function"), and his method of interpolation.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929194%E2%80%93195-212) He found an [orbital resonance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_resonance#Coincidental_'near'_ratios_of_mean_motion "Orbital resonance") with [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter") in proportion 18:7 in 1812; Gauss gave this result as [cipher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cipher "Cipher"), and gave the explicit meaning only in letters to Olbers and Bessel.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929206-213)[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-214)[[u]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-216) After long years of work, he finished it in 1816 without a result that seemed sufficient to him. This marked the end of his activities in theoretical astronomy.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929254-217)

[![Image 31](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Goettingen_Sternwarte_Besemann.png/250px-Goettingen_Sternwarte_Besemann.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Goettingen_Sternwarte_Besemann.png)

Göttingen observatory seen from the North-west (by Friedrich Besemann, c. 1835)

One fruit of Gauss's research on Pallas perturbations was the _Determinatio Attractionis..._ (1818) on a method of theoretical astronomy that later became known as the "elliptic ring method". It introduced an averaging conception in which a planet in orbit is replaced by a fictitious ring with mass density proportional to the time the planet takes to follow the corresponding orbital arcs.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929253%E2%80%93254-218) Gauss presents the method of evaluating the gravitational attraction of such an elliptic ring, which includes several steps; one of them involves a direct application of the arithmetic-geometric mean (AGM) algorithm to calculate an [elliptic integral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptic_integral "Elliptic integral").[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger1933169%E2%80%93170-219)

Even after Gauss's contributions to theoretical astronomy came to an end, more practical activities in [observational astronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observational_astronomy "Observational astronomy") continued and occupied him during his entire career. As early as 1799, Gauss dealt with the determination of longitude by use of the lunar parallax, for which he developed more convenient formulas than those were in common use.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel19298%E2%80%939-220) After appointment as director of observatory he attached importance to the fundamental astronomical constants in correspondence with Bessel. Gauss himself provided tables of [nutation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_nutation "Astronomical nutation") and [aberration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aberration_(astronomy) "Aberration (astronomy)"), solar coordinates, and refraction.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel19293-221) He made many contributions to [spherical geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_geometry "Spherical geometry"), and in this context solved some practical problems about [navigation by stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_navigation "Celestial navigation").[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel192954-222) He published a great number of observations, mainly on minor planets and comets; his last observation was the [solar eclipse of 28 July 1851](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Solar_eclipse_of_28_July_1851&action=edit&redlink=1 "Solar eclipse of 28 July 1851 (page does not exist)").[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929144-223)

Gauss's first publication following his doctoral thesis dealt with the determination of the [date of Easter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Date_of_Easter#Gauss's_Easter_algorithm "Date of Easter") (1800), an elementary mathematical topic. Gauss aimed to present a convenient algorithm for people without any knowledge of ecclesiastical or even astronomical chronology, and thus avoided the usual terms of [golden number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_number_(time) "Golden number (time)"), [epact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epact "Epact"), [solar cycle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_cycle_(calendar) "Solar cycle (calendar)"), [domenical letter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dominical_letter "Dominical letter"), and any religious connotations.[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaennchen193049%E2%80%9363-224) This choice of topic likely had historical grounds. The replacement of the [Julian calendar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Julian_calendar "Julian calendar") by the [Gregorian calendar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gregorian_calendar "Gregorian calendar") had caused confusion in the [Holy Roman Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire "Holy Roman Empire") since the 16th century and was not finished in Germany until 1700, when the difference of eleven days was deleted. Even after this, Easter fell on different dates in Protestant and Catholic territories, until this difference was abolished by agreement in 1776. In the Protestant states, such as the Duchy of Brunswick, the Easter of 1777, five weeks before Gauss's birth, was the first one calculated in the new manner.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Olesko-225)

Gauss likely used the [method of least squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_squares "Least squares") to minimize the impact of [measurement error](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observational_error "Observational error") when calculating the orbit of Ceres.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stigler-105) The method was published first by [Adrien-Marie Legendre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre "Adrien-Marie Legendre") in 1805, but Gauss claimed in _Theoria motus_ (1809) that he had been using it since 1794 or 1795.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaaf196484-226)[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-:3-227)[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-228) In the history of statistics, this disagreement is called the "priority dispute over the discovery of the method of least squares".[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Stigler-105) Gauss proved that the method has the lowest sampling variance within the class of linear unbiased estimators under the assumption of [normally distributed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normal_distribution "Normal distribution") errors ([Gauss–Markov theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Markov_theorem "Gauss–Markov theorem")), in the two-part paper _Theoria combinationis observationum erroribus minimis obnoxiae_ (1823).[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-229)

In the first paper he proved [Gauss's inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_inequality "Gauss's inequality") (a [Chebyshev-type inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chebyshev%27s_inequality "Chebyshev's inequality")) for [unimodal distributions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unimodality "Unimodality"), and stated without proof another inequality for [moments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moment_(mathematics) "Moment (mathematics)") of the fourth order (a special case of the Gauss-Winckler inequality).[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-230) He derived lower and upper bounds for the [variance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variance "Variance") of the [sample variance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sample_variance "Sample variance"). In the second paper, Gauss described [recursive least squares methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recursive_least_squares_filter "Recursive least squares filter"). His work on the theory of errors was extended in several directions by the geodesist [Friedrich Robert Helmert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Robert_Helmert "Friedrich Robert Helmert") to the [Gauss-Helmert model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss-Helmert_model "Gauss-Helmert model").[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-231)

Gauss also contributed to problems in [probability theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Probability_theory "Probability theory") that are not directly concerned with the theory of errors. One example appears as a diary note where he tried to describe the asymptotic distribution of entries in the continued fraction expansion of a random number uniformly distributed in _(0,1)_. He derived this distribution, now known as the [Gauss-Kuzmin distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss-Kuzmin_distribution "Gauss-Kuzmin distribution"), as a by-product of the discovery of the [ergodicity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ergodicity "Ergodicity") of the [Gauss map for continued fractions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss-Kuzmin-Wirsing_operator "Gauss-Kuzmin-Wirsing operator"). Gauss's solution is the first-ever result in the metrical theory of continued fractions.[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-232)

[![Image 32](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Georg_IV_Erlass_Landvermessung.jpg/250px-Georg_IV_Erlass_Landvermessung.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Georg_IV_Erlass_Landvermessung.jpg)

Order of King [George IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_IV "George IV") from 9 May 1820 to the triangulation project (with the additional signature of Count [Ernst zu Münster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ernst_zu_M%C3%BCnster "Ernst zu Münster") below)

[![Image 33](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Gau%C3%9F_Heliotrop%4020170430.JPG/250px-Gau%C3%9F_Heliotrop%4020170430.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gau%C3%9F_Heliotrop@20170430.JPG)

The [heliotrope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliotrope_(instrument) "Heliotrope (instrument)")

[![Image 34](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Vize-Heliotrop_Gau%C3%9F-Ausstellung_Bomann-Museum_%281%29.jpg/250px-Vize-Heliotrop_Gau%C3%9F-Ausstellung_Bomann-Museum_%281%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Vize-Heliotrop_Gau%C3%9F-Ausstellung_Bomann-Museum_(1).jpg)

Gauss's vice heliotrope, a [Troughton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Troughton "Edward Troughton") sextant with additional mirror

Gauss was busy with geodetic problems since 1799 when he helped [Karl Ludwig von Lecoq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karl_Ludwig_von_Lecoq "Karl Ludwig von Lecoq") with calculations during his [survey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surveying "Surveying") in [Westphalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Westphalia "Westphalia").[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192416%E2%80%9318-233) Beginning in 1804, he taught himself some practical geodesy in Brunswick[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192422-234) and Göttingen.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192428-235)

Since 1816, Gauss's former student [Heinrich Christian Schumacher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heinrich_Christian_Schumacher "Heinrich Christian Schumacher"), then professor in [Copenhagen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copenhagen "Copenhagen"), but living in [Altona](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altona,_Hamburg "Altona, Hamburg") ([Holstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duchy_of_Holstein "Duchy of Holstein")) near [Hamburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamburg "Hamburg") as head of an observatory, carried out a [triangulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangulation "Triangulation") of the [Jutland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jutland "Jutland") peninsula from [Skagen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skagen "Skagen") in the north to [Lauenburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lauenburg "Lauenburg") in the south.[[v]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-236) This project was the basis for map production but also aimed at determining the geodetic arc between the terminal sites. Data from geodetic arcs were used to determine the dimensions of the earth [geoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoid "Geoid"), and long arc distances brought more precise results. Schumacher asked Gauss to continue this work further to the south in the Kingdom of Hanover; Gauss agreed after a short time of hesitation. Finally, in May 1820, King [George IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_IV "George IV") gave the order to Gauss.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192432-237)

An [arc measurement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arc_measurement "Arc measurement") needs a precise astronomical determination of at least two points in the [network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangulation_network "Triangulation network"). Gauss and Schumacher used the coincidence that both observatories in Göttingen and Altona, in the garden of Schumacher's house, laid nearly in the same [longitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Longitude "Longitude"). The [latitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitude "Latitude") was measured with both their instruments and a [zenith sector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zenith_sector "Zenith sector") of [Ramsden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jesse_Ramsden "Jesse Ramsden") that was transported to both observatories.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192461-238)[[w]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-239)

Gauss and Schumacher had already determined some angles between [Lüneburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg "Lüneburg"), Hamburg, and Lauenburg for the geodetic connection in October 1818.[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192460-240) During the summers of 1821 until 1825 Gauss directed the triangulation work personally, from [Thuringia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thuringia "Thuringia") in the south to the river [Elbe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elbe "Elbe") in the north. The [triangle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangle "Triangle") between [Hoher Hagen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoher_Hagen_(Dransfeld) "Hoher Hagen (Dransfeld)"), [Großer Inselsberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gro%C3%9Fer_Inselsberg "Großer Inselsberg") in the [Thuringian Forest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thuringian_Forest "Thuringian Forest"), and [Brocken](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brocken "Brocken") in the [Harz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harz "Harz") mountains was the largest one Gauss had ever measured with a maximum size of 107 km (66.5 miles). In the thinly populated [Lüneburg Heath](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%BCneburg_Heath "Lüneburg Heath") without significant natural summits or artificial buildings, he had difficulties finding suitable triangulation points; sometimes cutting lanes through the vegetation was necessary.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Olesko-225)[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192475%E2%80%9380-241)

For pointing signals, Gauss invented a new instrument with movable mirrors and a small telescope that reflects the sunbeams to the triangulation points, and named it _[heliotrope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heliotrope\_(instrument) "Heliotrope (instrument)")_.[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaaf196481-242) Another suitable construction for the same purpose was a [sextant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sextant "Sextant") with an additional mirror which he named _vice heliotrope_.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192469-243) Gauss was assisted by soldiers of the Hanoverian army, among them his eldest son Joseph. Gauss took part in the [baseline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baseline_(surveying) "Baseline (surveying)") measurement ([Braak Base Line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braak_Base_Line "Braak Base Line")) of Schumacher in the village of [Braak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Braak,_Schleswig-Holstein "Braak, Schleswig-Holstein") near Hamburg in 1820, and used the result for the evaluation of the Hanoverian triangulation.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004121-244)

An additional result was a better value for the [flattening](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flattening "Flattening") of the approximative [Earth ellipsoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_ellipsoid "Earth ellipsoid").[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192437%E2%80%9338,_49%E2%80%9350-245)[[x]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-247) Gauss developed the [universal transverse Mercator projection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transverse_Mercator_projection#Ellipsoidal_transverse_Mercator "Transverse Mercator projection") of the ellipsoidal shaped Earth (what he named _conform projection_)[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004164-248) for representing geodetical data in plane charts.

When the arc measurement was finished, Gauss began the enlargement of the triangulation to the west to get a survey of the whole [Kingdom of Hanover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hanover "Kingdom of Hanover") with a Royal decree from 25 March 1828.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004135-249) The practical work was directed by three army officers, among them Lieutenant Joseph Gauss. The complete data evaluation laid in the hands of Gauss, who applied his mathematical inventions such as the [method of least squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Method_of_least_squares "Method of least squares") and the [elimination method](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_elimination "Gaussian elimination") to it. The project was finished in 1844, and Gauss sent a final report of the project to the government; his method of projection was not edited until 1866.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle1924129-250)[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-251)

In 1828, when studying differences in [latitude](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latitude "Latitude"), Gauss first defined a physical approximation for the [figure of the Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth "Figure of the Earth") as the surface everywhere perpendicular to the direction of gravity;[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Gau%C3%9F1828-252) later his doctoral student [Johann Benedict Listing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Benedict_Listing "Johann Benedict Listing") called this the _[geoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoid "Geoid")_.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-253)

### Magnetism and telegraphy

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carl_Friedrich_Gauss&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Magnetism and telegraphy")]

[![Image 35](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/G%C3%B6ttingen-Gau%C3%9F-Weber-Monument.01.JPG/250px-G%C3%B6ttingen-Gau%C3%9F-Weber-Monument.01.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:G%C3%B6ttingen-Gau%C3%9F-Weber-Monument.01.JPG)

Gauss-Weber monument in Göttingen by Ferdinand Hartzer (1899)

[![Image 36](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/A_magnetometer_used_by_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss%2C_from_Gerlach_und_F._Traum%C3%BCller%2C_1899.png/250px-A_magnetometer_used_by_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss%2C_from_Gerlach_und_F._Traum%C3%BCller%2C_1899.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:A_magnetometer_used_by_Carl_Friedrich_Gauss,_from_Gerlach_und_F._Traum%C3%BCller,_1899.png)

The Gauss–Weber magnetometer

Gauss had been interested in magnetism since 1803.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004153-254) After [Alexander von Humboldt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt "Alexander von Humboldt") visited Göttingen in 1826, both scientists began intensive research on [geomagnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geomagnetism "Geomagnetism"), partly independently, partly in productive cooperation.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-255) In 1828, Gauss was Humboldt's guest during the conference of the [Society of German Natural Scientists and Physicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Society_of_German_Natural_Scientists_and_Physicians "Society of German Natural Scientists and Physicians") in Berlin, where he got acquainted with the physicist [Wilhelm Weber](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber "Wilhelm Eduard Weber").[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004136-256)

When Weber got the chair for physics in Göttingen as successor of [Johann Tobias Mayer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Tobias_Mayer "Johann Tobias Mayer") by Gauss's recommendation in 1831, both of them started a fruitful collaboration, leading to a new knowledge of [magnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetism "Magnetism") with a representation for the unit of magnetism in terms of mass, charge, and time.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004161-257) They founded the _Magnetic Association_ (German: _Magnetischer Verein_), an international working group of several observatories, which carried out measurements of [Earth's magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field "Earth's magnetic field") in many regions of the world using equivalent methods at arranged dates in the years 1836 to 1841.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich-258)

In 1836, Humboldt suggested the establishment of a worldwide net of geomagnetic stations in the [British dominions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Empire "British Empire") with a letter to the [Duke of Sussex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prince_Augustus_Frederick,_Duke_of_Sussex "Prince Augustus Frederick, Duke of Sussex"), then president of the Royal Society; he proposed that magnetic measures should be taken under standardized conditions using his methods.[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-259)[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-260) Together with other instigators, this led to a global program known as "[Magnetical crusade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Sabine#Magnetical_crusade "Edward Sabine")" under the direction of [Edward Sabine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edward_Sabine "Edward Sabine"). The dates, times, and intervals of observations were determined in advance, the _Göttingen mean time_ was used as the standard.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Rupke-261) 61 stations on all five continents participated in this global program. Gauss and Weber founded a series for publication of the results, six volumes were edited between 1837 and 1843. Weber's departure to [Leipzig](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leipzig_University "Leipzig University") in 1843 as late effect of the [Göttingen Seven affair](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Seven "Göttingen Seven") marked the end of Magnetic Association activity.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich-258)

Following Humboldt's example, Gauss ordered a magnetic [observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observatory "Observatory") to be built in the garden of the observatory, but the scientists differed over instrumental equipment; Gauss preferred stationary instruments, which he thought to give more precise results, whereas Humboldt was accustomed to movable instruments. Gauss was interested in the temporal and spatial variation of magnetic [declination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_declination "Magnetic declination"), [inclination](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_dip "Magnetic dip"), and intensity and differentiated, unlike Humboldt, between "horizontal" and "vertical" intensity. Together with Weber, he developed methods of measuring the components of the intensity of the magnetic field and constructed a suitable [magnetometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetometer "Magnetometer") to measure _absolute values_ of the strength of the Earth's magnetic field, not more relative ones that depended on the apparatus.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich-258)[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaaf1964115%E2%80%93127-262) The precision of the magnetometer was about ten times higher than that of previous instruments. With this work, Gauss was the first to derive a non-mechanical quantity by basic mechanical quantities.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Rupke-261)

Gauss carried out a _General Theory of Terrestrial Magnetism_ (1839), in what he believed to describe the nature of magnetic force; according to Felix Klein, this work is a presentation of observations by use of [spherical harmonics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_harmonics "Spherical harmonics") rather than a physical theory.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197921%E2%80%9323-263) The theory predicted the existence of exactly two [magnetic poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies#Magnetic_poles "Poles of astronomical bodies") on the Earth, thus [Hansteen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Hansteen "Christopher Hansteen")'s idea of four magnetic poles became obsolete,[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Roussanova-264) and the data allowed to determine their location with rather good precision.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer192987-265)

Gauss influenced the beginning of geophysics in Russia, when [Adolph Theodor Kupffer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adolph_Theodor_Kupffer "Adolph Theodor Kupffer"), one of his former students, founded a magnetic observatory in [St. Petersburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/St._Petersburg "St. Petersburg"), following the example of the observatory in Göttingen, and similarly, [Ivan Simonov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivan_Simonov "Ivan Simonov") in [Kazan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kazan "Kazan").[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Roussanova-264)

[![Image 37](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gauss-Weber-Telegraf_Paulinerkirche_02.jpg/250px-Gauss-Weber-Telegraf_Paulinerkirche_02.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gauss-Weber-Telegraf_Paulinerkirche_02.jpg)

Town plan of Göttingen with course of the telegraphic connection

The discoveries of [Hans Christian Ørsted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted "Hans Christian Ørsted") on [electromagnetism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetism "Electromagnetism") and [Michael Faraday](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Faraday "Michael Faraday") on [electromagnetic induction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction "Electromagnetic induction") drew Gauss's attention to these matters.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer19296-266) Gauss and Weber found rules for branched [electric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electricity "Electricity") circuits, which were later found independently and first published by [Gustav Kirchhoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gustav_Kirchhoff "Gustav Kirchhoff") and named after him as [Kirchhoff's circuit laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_circuit_laws "Kirchhoff's circuit laws"),[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer1929108-267) and made inquiries into electromagnetism. They constructed the first [electromechanical telegraph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_telegraph "Electrical telegraph") in 1833, and Weber himself connected the observatory with the institute for physics in the town centre of Göttingen,[[y]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-269) but they made no further commercial use of this invention.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Timm-268)[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-270)

Gauss's main theoretical interests in electromagnetism were reflected in his attempts to formulate quantitive laws governing electromagnetic induction. In notebooks from these years, he recorded several innovative formulations; he discovered the [vector potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_potential "Vector potential") function, independently rediscovered by [Franz Ernst Neumann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Ernst_Neumann "Franz Ernst Neumann") in 1845, and in January 1835 he wrote down an "induction law" equivalent to [Faraday's law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faraday%27s_law_of_induction "Faraday's law of induction"), which stated that the [electromotive force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromotive_force "Electromotive force") at a given point in space is equal to the [instantaneous rate of change](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instantaneous_rate_of_change "Instantaneous rate of change") (with respect to time) of this function.[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-271)[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-272)

Gauss tried to find a unifying law for long-distance effects of [electrostatics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrostatics "Electrostatics"), [electrodynamics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrodynamics "Electrodynamics"), electromagnetism, and [induction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_Induction "Electric Induction"), comparable to Newton's law of gravitation,[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer1929148%E2%80%93152-273) but his attempt ended in a "tragic failure".[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Rupke-261)

Since Isaac Newton had shown theoretically that the Earth and rotating stars assume non-spherical shapes, the problem of attraction of ellipsoids gained importance in mathematical astronomy. In his first publication on potential theory, the "Theoria attractionis..." (1813), Gauss provided a [closed-form expression](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed-form_expression "Closed-form expression") to the gravitational attraction of a homogeneous [triaxial ellipsoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triaxial_ellipsoid "Triaxial ellipsoid") at every point in space.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeppert193332-274) In contrast to previous research of [Maclaurin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colin_Maclaurin "Colin Maclaurin"), Laplace and Lagrange, Gauss's new solution treated the attraction more directly in the form of an elliptic integral. In the process, he also proved and applied some special cases of the so-called [Gauss's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergence_theorem "Divergence theorem") in [vector analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_analysis "Vector analysis").[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeppert193332%E2%80%9340-275)

In the _General theorems concerning the attractive and repulsive forces acting in reciprocal proportions of quadratic distances_ (1840) Gauss gave a basic theory of [magnetic potential](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_vector_potential "Magnetic vector potential"), based on Lagrange, Laplace, and Poisson;[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlein197921%E2%80%9323-263) it seems rather unlikely that he knew the previous works of [George Green](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Green_(mathematician) "George Green (mathematician)") on this subject.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer19296-266) However, Gauss could never give any reasons for magnetism, nor a theory of magnetism similar to Newton's work on gravitation, that enabled scientists to predict geomagnetic effects in the future.[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Rupke-261)

Gauss's calculations enabled instrument maker [Johann Georg Repsold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Georg_Repsold "Johann Georg Repsold") in [Hamburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hamburg "Hamburg") to construct a new [achromatic lens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Achromatic_lens "Achromatic lens") system in 1810. A main problem, among other difficulties, was that the [refractive index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refractive_index "Refractive index") and [dispersion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dispersion_(optics) "Dispersion (optics)") of the glass used were not precisely known.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer1929153%E2%80%93154-276) In a short article from 1817 Gauss dealt with the problem of removal of [chromatic aberration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chromatic_aberration "Chromatic aberration") in [double lenses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_lens "Gauss lens"), and computed adjustments of the shape and coefficients of refraction required to minimize it. His work was noted by the optician [Carl August von Steinheil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_August_von_Steinheil "Carl August von Steinheil"), who in 1860 introduced the achromatic [Steinheil doublet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Achromatic_lens "Achromatic lens"), partly based on Gauss's calculations.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer1929159%E2%80%93165-277) Many results in [geometrical optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometrical_optics "Geometrical optics") are scattered in Gauss's correspondences and hand notes.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004170-278)

In the _Dioptrical Investigations_ (1840), Gauss gave the first systematic analysis of the formation of images under a [paraxial approximation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraxial_approximation "Paraxial approximation") ([Gaussian optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_optics "Gaussian optics")).[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Hecht-279) He characterized optical systems under a paraxial approximation only by its [cardinal points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cardinal_point_(optics) "Cardinal point (optics)"),[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Bass-280) and he derived the Gaussian [lens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lens "Lens") formula, applicable without restrictions in respect to the thickness of the lenses.[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Ostdiek-281)[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer1929189%E2%80%93208-282)

Gauss's first work in mechanics concerned the [earth's rotation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation "Earth's rotation"). When his university friend [Benzenberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Benzenberg "Johann Benzenberg") carried out experiments to determine the deviation of falling masses from the perpendicular in 1802, what today is known as the [Coriolis force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coriolis_force "Coriolis force"), he asked Gauss for a theory-based calculation of the values for comparison with the experimental ones. Gauss elaborated a system of fundamental equations for the motion, and the results corresponded sufficiently with Benzenberg's data, who added Gauss's considerations as an appendix to his book on falling experiments.[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeppert19333%E2%80%9311-283)

After [Foucault](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault "Léon Foucault") had demonstrated the earth's rotation by his [pendulum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foucault_pendulum "Foucault pendulum") experiment in public in 1851, Gerling questioned Gauss for further explanations. This instigated Gauss to design a new apparatus for demonstration with a much shorter length of pendulum than Foucault's one. The oscillations were observed with a reading telescope, with a vertical scale and a mirror fastened at the pendulum. It is described in the Gauss–Gerling correspondence and Weber made some experiments with this apparatus in 1853, but no data were published.[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeppert193312-16-284)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-285)

[Gauss's principle of least constraint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss%27s_principle_of_least_constraint "Gauss's principle of least constraint") of 1829 was established as a general concept to overcome the division of mechanics into statics and dynamics, combining [D'Alembert's principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_principle "D'Alembert's principle") with [Lagrange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph-Louis_Lagrange "Joseph-Louis Lagrange")'s [principle of virtual work](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principle_of_virtual_work "Principle of virtual work"), and showing analogies to the method of [least squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_squares "Least squares").[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeppert193316-26-286)

In 1828, Gauss was appointed as head of the board for weights and measures of the Kingdom of Hanover. He created [standards](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_(metrology) "Standard (metrology)") for length and measure. Gauss himself took care of the time-consuming measures and gave detailed orders for the mechanical construction.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Olesko-225) In the correspondence with Schumacher, who was also working on this matter, he described new ideas for high-precision scales.[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeppert193359%E2%80%9360-287) He submitted the final reports on the Hanoverian [foot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foot_(unit) "Foot (unit)") and [pound](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pound_(mass) "Pound (mass)") to the government in 1841. This work achieved international importance due to an 1836 law that connected the Hanoverian measures with the English ones.[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Olesko-225)

[![Image 38](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_Copley-Medaille%2C_1838.jpg/250px-Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F%2C_Copley-Medaille%2C_1838.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Carl_Friedrich_Gau%C3%9F,_Copley-Medaille,_1838.jpg)

[Copley Medal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copley_Medal "Copley Medal") for Gauss (1838)

Gauss first became member of a scientific society, the [Russian Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences "Russian Academy of Sciences"), in 1802.[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004351-288) Further memberships (corresponding, foreign or full) were awarded by the [Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Humanities "Göttingen Academy of Sciences and Humanities") in Göttingen (1802/ 1807),[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-289) the [French Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences "French Academy of Sciences") (1804/ 1820),[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-290) the [Royal Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society "Royal Society") of London (1804),[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-291) the [Royal Prussian Academy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prussian_Academy_of_Sciences "Prussian Academy of Sciences") in Berlin (1810),[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-292) the [National Academy of Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accademia_nazionale_delle_scienze "Accademia nazionale delle scienze") in Verona (1810),[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-293) the [Royal Society of Edinburgh](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Edinburgh "Royal Society of Edinburgh") (1820),[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-294) the [Bavarian Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bavarian_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Humanities "Bavarian Academy of Sciences and Humanities") of Munich (1820),[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-295) the [Royal Danish Academy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Danish_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Letters "Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters") in Copenhagen (1821),[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004352-296) the [Royal Astronomical Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Astronomical_Society "Royal Astronomical Society") in London (1821),[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-297) the [Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences "Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences") (1821),[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004352-296) the [American Academy of Arts and Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences "American Academy of Arts and Sciences") in Boston (1822),[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-AAAS-298) the [Royal Bohemian Society of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Bohemian_Society_of_Sciences "Royal Bohemian Society of Sciences") in Prague (1833),[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004353-299) the [Royal Academy of Science, Letters and Fine Arts of Belgium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Academy_of_Science,_Letters_and_Fine_Arts_of_Belgium "Royal Academy of Science, Letters and Fine Arts of Belgium") (1841/1845),[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-300) the [Royal Society of Sciences in Uppsala](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Society_of_Sciences_in_Uppsala the [Royal Institute of the Netherlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences "Royal Netherlands Academy of Arts and Sciences") (1845/ Academy of Sciences") in Madrid (1850),[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-302) the [Russian Geographical Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russian_Geographical_Society "Russian Geographical Society") Philosophical Society](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambridge_Philosophical_Society "Cambridge Philosophical Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koninklijke_Hollandsche_Maatschappij_der_Wetenschappen "Koninklijke Hollandsche Maatschappij der Wetenschappen") in Haarlem.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004351%E2%80%93355-305)[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004359-306)

Both the [University of Kazan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Kazan "University of Kazan") and the Philosophy Faculty of the [University of Prague](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_University "Charles University") appointed him honorary member in 1848.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004351%E2%80%93355-305)

Gauss received the [Lalande Prize](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lalande_Prize "Lalande Prize") from the French Academy of Science in 1809 for the theory of planets and the means of determining their orbits from only three observations,[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-307) the Danish Academy of Science prize in 1823 for his memoir on conformal projection,[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004353-299) and the [Copley Medal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copley_Medal "Copley Medal") from the Royal Society in 1838 for "his inventions and mathematical researches in magnetism".[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004359-306)[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-308)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Wittmann-41)

Gauss was appointed Knight of the French [Legion of Honour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Legion_of_Honour "Legion of Honour") in 1837,[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-309) and became one of the first members of the Prussian [Order Pour le Merite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_Pour_le_Merite#Civil_class "Order Pour le Merite") (Civil class) when it was established in 1842.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-310) He received the [Order of the Crown of Westphalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_the_Crown_of_Westphalia "Order of the Crown of Westphalia") (1810),[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004359-306) the Danish [Order of the Dannebrog](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_the_Dannebrog "Order of the Dannebrog") (1817),[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004359-306) the Hanoverian [Royal Guelphic Order](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Royal_Guelphic_Order "Royal Guelphic Order") (1815),[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004359-306) the Swedish [Order of the Polar Star](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_the_Polar_Star "Order of the Polar Star") (1844),[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004354-311) the [Order of Henry the Lion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_of_Henry_the_Lion "Order of Henry the Lion") (1849),[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004354-311) and the [Bavarian Maximilian Order for Science and Art](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bavarian_Maximilian_Order_for_Science_and_Art "Bavarian Maximilian Order for Science and Art") (1853).[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004355-303)

The Kings of Hanover appointed him the honorary titles "[Hofrath](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hofrath "Hofrath")" (1816)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004288-66) and "Geheimer Hofrath"[[z]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-312) (1845). In 1949, on the occasion of his golden doctor degree jubilee, he received [honorary citizenship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honorary_citizenship "Honorary citizenship") of both Brunswick and Göttingen.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004355-303) Soon after his death a medal was issued by order of King [George V of Hanover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_V_of_Hanover "George V of Hanover") with the back inscription dedicated "to the Prince of Mathematicians".[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWu%C3%9Fing198286-313)

The "Gauss-Gesellschaft Göttingen" ("Göttingen Gauss Society") was founded in 1964 for research on the life and work of Carl Friedrich Gauss and related persons. It publishes the _Mitteilungen der Gauss-Gesellschaft_ (_Communications of the Gauss Society_).[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-314)

The [Göttingen Academy of Sciences and Humanities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Humanities "Göttingen Academy of Sciences and Humanities") provides a complete collection of the known letters from and to Carl Friedrich Gauss that is accessible online.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Correspondence-43) The literary estate is kept and provided by the [Göttingen State and University Library](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_State_and_University_Library "Göttingen State and University Library").[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-316) Written materials from Carl Friedrich Gauss and family members can also be found in the municipal archive of Brunswick.[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-317)

1.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-10)**The _Collegium Carolinum_ was a preceding institution of the [Technical University of Braunschweig](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technical_University_of_Braunschweig "Technical University of Braunschweig"), but at Gauss's time not equal to a university.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200417-9)
2.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-16)**Once Gauss drew a lecture scene with professor Kästner producing errors in a simple calculation.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Ullrich-13)
3.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-25)**This error occurs for example in Marsden (1977).[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Marsden-24)
4.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-42)**Gauss announced 195 lectures, 70 per cent of them on astronomical, 15 per cent on mathematical, 9 per cent on geodetical, and 6 per cent on physical subjects.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Wittmann-41)
5.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-44)**The index of correspondence shows that Benjamin Gould was presumably the last correspondent who, on 13 February 1855, sent a letter to Gauss in his lifetime. It was an actual letter of farewell, but it is uncertain whether it reached the addressee just in time.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Correspondence-43)
6.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-47)**After his death, a discourse on the perturbations of Pallas in French was found among his papers, probably as a contribution to a prize competition of the French Academy of Science.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929211-46)
7.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-49)**The _Theoria motus..._ was completed in the German language in 1806, but on request of the editor [Friedrich Christoph Perthes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Christoph_Perthes "Friedrich Christoph Perthes") Gauss translated it into Latin.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200490-48)
8.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-55)**Both Gauss and Harding dropped only veiled hints on this personal problem in their correspondence. A letter to Schumacher indicates that Gauss tried to get rid of his colleague and searched for a new position for him outside of Göttingen, but without result. Apart from that, Charlotte Waldeck, Gauss's mother-in-law, pleaded with Olbers to try to provide Gauss with another position far from Göttingen.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-K%C3%BCssner-54)
9.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-58)**Gauss's first assistant was [Benjamin Goldschmidt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Wolfgang_Benjamin_Goldschmidt "Carl Wolfgang Benjamin Goldschmidt"), and his second [Wilhelm Klinkerfues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Klinkerfues "Wilhelm Klinkerfues"), who later became one of his successors.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Wittmann-41)
10.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-Bessel_62-0)**Bessel never got a university education.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-60)[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington200476-61)
11.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-82)**On this journey he met the geodesist [Ferdinand Rudolph Hassler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferdinand_Rudolph_Hassler "Ferdinand Rudolph Hassler"), who was a scientific correspondent of Carl Friedrich Gauss.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-80)[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004286-81)
12.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-85)**Following Bolyai's handwritten Hungarian text at the bottom, Gauss intentionally characterized Kästner with the added the wrong addition.
13.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-118)**The first book he loaned from the university library in 1795 was the novel _[Clarissa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clarissa;\_or,\_The\_History\_of\_a\_Young\_Lady "Clarissa; or, The History of a Young Lady")_ from [Samuel Richardson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samuel_Richardson "Samuel Richardson").[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-117)
14.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-124)**The political background was the confusing situation of the [German Confederation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_Confederation "German Confederation") with 39 nearly independent states, the sovereigns of three of them being Kings of other countries (Netherlands, Danmark, United Kingdom), whereas the [Kingdom of Prussia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia "Kingdom of Prussia") and the [Austrian Empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austrian_Empire "Austrian Empire") extended widely over the frontiers of the Confederation.
15.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-144)**Gauss told the story later in detail in a letter to [Encke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Franz_Encke "Johann Franz Encke").[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBachmann19224-143)
16.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-158)**Later, these transformations were given by Legendre in 1824 (3th order), Jacobi in 1829 (5th order), [Sohncke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Adolf_Sohncke "Ludwig Adolf Sohncke") in 1837 (7th and other orders).
17.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-160)**In a letter to Bessel from 1828, Gauss commented: "Mr. Abel has [...] anticipated me, and relieves me of the effort [of publishing] in respect to one third of these matters ..."[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchlesinger193341-159)
18.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-164)**This remark appears at article 335 of chapter 7 of _Disquisitiones Arithmeticae_ (1801).
19.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-190)**Hesemann also took a death mask from Gauss.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunnington2004324-51)
20.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-209)**The unambiguous identification of a cosmic object as planet among the fixed stars requires at least two observations with interval.
21.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-216)**Brendel (1929) thought this cipher to be insoluble, but actually decoding was very easy.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrendel1929206-213)[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-215)
22.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-236)**Lauenburg was the southernmost town of the [Duchy of Holstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duchy_of_Holstein "Duchy of Holstein"), that was held in personal union by the King of [Denmark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denmark "Denmark").
23.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-239)**This Ramsden sector was loaned by the [Board of Ordnance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Board_of_Ordnance "Board of Ordnance"), and had earlier been used by [William Mudge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Mudge "William Mudge") in the [Principal Triangulation of Great Britain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principal_Triangulation_of_Great_Britain "Principal Triangulation of Great Britain").[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192461-238)
24.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-247)**The value from [Walbeck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henrik_Johan_Walbeck "Henrik Johan Walbeck") (1820) of 1/302,78 was improved to 1/298.39; the calculation was done by Eduard Schmidt, private lecturer at Göttingen University.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalle192449-50-246)
25.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-269)**A thunderstorm damaged the cable in 1845.[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Timm-268)
26.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-312)**literally translation: _Secrete Councillor of the Court_
27.   **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_ref-315)**Gauss presented the text to the Göttingen Academy in December 1832, a preprint in Latin with a small number of copies appeared in 1833. It was soon translated and published in German and French. The complete text in Latin was published in 1841.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss#cite_note-Reich-258)
